\begin{itemize}

\[

\[
x_1^2 + x_2^2 + \cdots + x_n^2 \le n.
\]
\end{itemize}
If a player cannot choose a suitable $x_n$, the game ends and the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither player wins. All chosen numbers are known to both players.
Determine all values of $\lambda$ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

</self_evaluation>
<score>S</score>

where S is exactly one of 0, 0.5, or 1 (nothing else inside the score tag):
- 1: completely correct, all steps justified.
- 0.5: generally correct, but minor gaps or omissions remain.
- 0: fatal errors, severe omissions, or does not solve the problem.<｜Assistant｜><think>

- If n is even (Bazza's turn), he chooses x_n ≥ 0 such that sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2 ≤ n.

We need to determine all λ for which Alice has a winning strategy, and all λ for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

Interpretation: The game ends when a player cannot make a move. The moves are constrained by cumulative sums: Alice's moves constrained by linear sum, Bazza's moves constrained by quadratic sum. Both players know all previous numbers. They choose nonnegative reals. The game can potentially go on forever.

Let S_n = sum_{i=1}^n x_i, and Q_n = sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2. The constraints: For odd n, Alice chooses x_n such that S_n ≤ λ n. For even n, Bazza chooses x_n such that Q_n ≤ n.

Note: The constraints are only upper bounds, not lower bounds. So players can choose small numbers.

The game ends when a player cannot choose any nonnegative x_n satisfying the constraint, given the previous numbers. We need to determine for each λ, which player has a winning strategy. It's a two-player zero-sum game with infinite horizon. The usual approach is to find "critical" value λ0 such that for λ > λ0, Alice can force a win, for λ < λ0, Bazza can force a win, and at λ = λ0, it's a draw (both can survive indefinitely). Thus the game is about who can force the opponent's cumulative sum to exceed their bound.

Let's analyze the game systematically using "critical" values. This is reminiscent of a "bootstrap" or "Pursuit" game where the winner is determined by a threshold.

We need to find all λ for which Alice has a winning strategy, and all λ for which Bazza has a winning strategy. Possibly there is a region where both can survive indefinitely (draw). But we need to determine if that region exists.

Let's define the game in terms of the state after each turn. Since the constraints are linear and quadratic, we might consider the possibility of "optimal" play where each player uses a "mirror" strategy or "constant" strategy.

One approach: This is a two-player game with perfect information and infinite horizon. The outcome can be determined by analyzing the "critical" value λ0 such that for λ > λ0, Alice can force a win, and for λ < λ0, Bazza can force a win. At λ = λ0, it might be a draw (both can survive forever). This is typical for such games.

We need to find λ0. Let's try to find if there is a "strategy" for Bazza to win for λ < 1. For λ < 1, we suspect Bazza can win by choosing numbers that cause Alice's linear sum to exceed λ n. For instance, consider λ = 0.5. Can Bazza force a win? Let's attempt to design a strategy.

Bazza wants to eventually make S_n > λ (n+1) at the end of his turn (i.e., after his move) so that Alice cannot move on her next odd turn. He can choose his moves based on previous numbers.

Observation: If Bazza always chooses x_n = 1 on his turns, then S_n grows by 1 each even turn. For λ=0.5, after his first turn (n=2), S_2 = x_1 + 1. If Alice chooses x_1 ≤ 0.5, say 0.5, then S_2=1.5. Then on her next turn n=3, she needs S_3 ≤ 1.5, so she can choose 0. Then S_3=1.5. Then Bazza's turn n=4: he chooses x_4=1, then S_4=2.5. Then Alice's turn n=5: she needs S_5 ≤ 2.5, so she can choose 0, S_5=2.5. Then Bazza's turn n=6: x_6=1, S_6=3.5. Then Alice's turn n=7: she needs S_7 ≤ 3.5, so she can choose 0, S_7=3.5. So far so good. But we need to check if Alice can ever be forced to exceed the bound. She always chooses 0, so S_n is exactly the sum of Bazza's moves. For λ=0.5, the bound for her at odd n is 0.5 n. At n=3, bound=1.5, S_3=1.5, okay. At n=5, bound=2.5, S_5=2.5, okay. At n=7, bound=3.5, S_7=3.5, okay. So she survives. In fact, if Bazza always chooses 1, then S_{2k-1} = x_1 + (k-1)*1? We need to determine if for λ < 1, Bazza can force a win. This is reminiscent of a "bootstrap" game where the bound for Bazza's quadratic sum is n, and he wants to eventually make S exceed λ n. Since λ<1, his allowed x_n can be up to sqrt(n - Q_n). For large n, he can choose large x_n if Q_n is small. But if he chooses large x_n, Q_n becomes large, limiting his future moves. So he needs to balance.

Perhaps we can think of the game in terms of "cumulative resource". Let's denote A_n = sum_{i=1}^n x_i, and B_n = sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2. The constraints: For odd n, A_n ≤ λ n; for even n, B_n ≤ n. The game ends when a player cannot make a move. So essentially, the players are trying to keep their own cumulative sums within a bound, but they also affect the opponent's bound.

We can attempt to find a "strategy stealing" or "mirror" strategy. Another approach: Consider the "value" of the game as a function of the state (A, B, n). Since the game is infinite, but we can consider the possibility of "optimal" play where both players try to survive indefinitely. The question is: For which λ can each player force a win?

We can try to find if there is a "critical" λ such that for λ > λ0, Alice can win by making Q grow faster than n, and for λ < λ0, Bazza can win by making A grow faster than λ n. At λ = λ0, they may be able to keep the game going forever.

Let's attempt to find the threshold. Consider the "worst-case" for Alice if she tries to keep Q small. She can always choose 0, so Q = sum of Bazza's squares. If Bazza wants to win, he wants to eventually make A > λ n on Alice's turn. He can choose his moves to increase A. However, he is constrained by B ≤ n. So he can choose x_n up to sqrt(n - B_{n-1}). If B_{n-1} is small, he can choose a large x_n. But he must also consider that his own moves affect future B constraints. So a possible strategy for Bazza is to always choose the maximum allowed x_n given his constraint, i.e., x_n = sqrt(n - B_{n-1}). This would make B_n = n. Then he uses all his allowed quadratic sum. Then the sum A_n increases by that amount each even turn. Then we can check if Alice can ever survive. For λ < 1, maybe this strategy forces her to lose.

Let's analyze the game as a kind of "bootstrap" where each player can influence the opponent's cumulative sum. This is reminiscent of a game where two players alternately add numbers, and each has a constraint on the sum of their own numbers and the opponent's squares. This is similar to the "inequality game" or "resource game".

We can try to think in terms of the "worst-case" for each player. Define the "score" after each turn. Since the constraints involve n, we might consider the "potential" function like A_n - λ n, and B_n - n.

Then A_4=0.9+1.786=2.686, Q_4=0.81+3.19=4.0. Then Alice's turn n=5: she needs S_5 ≤4.5, so she can choose x_5=4.5-2.686=1.814, then Q_5=4+3.29=7.29, then Bazza's turn n=6: he needs Q_6 ≤6, but Q_5=7.29>6, so he loses immediately. So if Bazza chooses x_2=0, then Alice can win by playing x_5 large? But Alice must also survive her own turn at n=5; she chooses x_5=1.814, which is allowed because S_4=2.686, and 2.686+1.814=4.5 ≤4.5, so she can do that. Then Q_5=7.29, and then Bazza loses at n=6. So Bazza should not choose x_2=0 because that gives Alice a winning move. So Bazza must choose x_2 such that Alice cannot win on her next turn. For λ=0.9, if he chooses x_2=0, then Alice can win. So he must choose a larger x_2 to make S_2 larger, so that S_4 is larger, reducing Alice's ability to choose a large x_5? Actually, Alice's maximum x_5 is 5λ - S_4. If S_4 is large, then her allowed x_5 is small, so she cannot make Q_5 too large. But she can also choose x_5=0, then Q_5=Q_4, and then Bazza's constraint at n=6 is Q_6 ≤6. If Q_4 <6, then he can survive by choosing x_6=0. But then later, she might cause trouble. So we need to analyze the game as a "bootstrap" where each player tries to survive.

Given the complexity, it might be easier to think of the game in terms of "critical λ" as the value where the "optimal" play leads to a draw. This is a known type of game: the "inequality game" where two players alternately choose nonnegative numbers, and each has a constraint on the sum of their own numbers and the sum of squares of the opponent. This is reminiscent of a problem from the International Mathematical Olympiad Shortlist or something. I recall a problem: "Alice and Bob play a game where on each turn, a player chooses a nonnegative real number such that the sum of all numbers chosen so far is at most n, and the sum of squares is at most n." Something like that. Actually, there is a known result: For λ ≤ 1, Bazza wins; for λ ≥ 1, Alice wins. But we need to verify.

Let's try to find a winning strategy for Bazza when λ < 1. Possibly he can adopt the strategy: At each even turn, he chooses x_n = 1 (if possible) or something, and then Alice's linear sum will eventually exceed λ n. But we saw that for λ=0.9, if he chooses 1 each time, Alice can survive by choosing 0 each time. However, if he chooses 1, then the sum S after his turn is x_1 + (k-1). For x_1=0.9, S_{2k}=0.9+(k-1)=k-0.1. The bound for Alice at odd n=2k+1 is 0.9(2k+1)=1.8k+0.9. So S_{2k} is less than that, so she can choose 0 and survive. So he needs to force her to be stuck at some point. Maybe he can choose a larger number on his turns if he keeps the quadratic sum low. For instance, if Alice chooses x_1=0.9, then Bazza can choose x_2 = sqrt(2 - 0.81)=1.091, which is the maximum. Then S_2=1.991. Then Alice's turn n=3: she can choose x_3=0, then S_3=1.991, Q_3=2.0. Then Alice's turn n=7: she can choose x_7 = 6.3 - 5.395 = 0.905, then S_7=6.3, Q_7=6+0.819=6.819. Then Bazza's turn n=8: he can choose x_8 = sqrt(8-6.819)=sqrt(1.181)=1.087, then S_8=7.387, Q_8=6.819+1.181=8.0. So pattern: after each pair, S increases by about 1.414, then 1.095, then 0.895, etc. So S_{2k} is roughly? Let's compute asymptotic: S_{2k} = x_1 + x_2 + sum_{i=4}^{2k} x_i. We have x_2=1.091, x_4=1.414, x_6=0.895, x_8=1.087, x_10? Not easy. The quadratic sum is exactly n after each even turn? Actually, in this strategy, we set B_n = n after each even turn? Not exactly: B_2=2, B_4=4, B_6=6, B_8=8, ... So B_{2k}=2k. That means that on each even turn, Bazza chooses x_{2k} = sqrt(2k - B_{2k-1}) = sqrt(2k - (2k-2 + x_{2k-1}^2))? Actually, B_{2k-1}=B_{2k-2}+x_{2k-1}^2. If B_{2k-2}=2k-2, then B_{2k-1}=2k-2+x_{2k-1}^2. Then x_{2k}=sqrt(2k - (2k-2+x_{2k-1}^2)) = sqrt(2 - x_{2k-1}^2). So his move depends on Alice's previous move. So if Alice chooses x_{2k-1}=0, then x_{2k}=sqrt(2)≈1.414. So after the first few, his moves are 1.414. But we saw that after n=6, x_6=0.895 because Alice chose x_5=1.095, which gave x_6=sqrt(2-1.095^2)=sqrt(2-1.199)=sqrt(0.801)=0.895. So the moves are not constant; they depend on Alice's choices.

Thus, the game is dynamic; each player can choose their moves to affect the opponent's future moves.

To determine the winner, we need to find if either player can force a win. This is a typical "deterministic" two-player game with infinite horizon. We can think of it as a "pursuit" where each player's cumulative sum must stay below a linear bound, but the opponent's cumulative quadratic sum must stay below n.

We can attempt to analyze using "strategy stealing" or "strategy" based on "mirror" moves. Perhaps we can consider the following: For any λ, we can define the "critical" value λ0 = 1. For λ < 1, Bazza can adopt a strategy to always choose x_n = 1 for all even n, and then show that Alice will eventually be forced to exceed λ n. Thus, for λ=0.9, Bazza cannot kill her with the maximum strategy. But perhaps he can win by a different strategy that makes his moves larger initially, even if that means his quadratic sum becomes large, but later he can still survive? But if he makes large moves, his quadratic sum increases, which limits his future moves. So the optimal strategy for Bazza is to use a "balancing" approach: he wants to eventually cause Alice to be stuck, so he wants to make A_n large relative to λ n. He can influence A_n by his own moves, but those moves also affect Q_n. So he needs to maximize the growth of A_n while keeping Q_n within n. This is like a "control" problem.

We can think of the game as a two-player game where each player's move is a function of the state. This is similar to the "inequality game" that appears in some olympiad problems. I recall a problem: "Alice and Bob play a game where on each turn, a player chooses a nonnegative real number. Alice's constraint: sum of all numbers so far ≤ λ n. Bob's constraint: sum of squares ≤ n. Determine all λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and for which Bob has a winning strategy." The answer is: Alice wins for λ > 1, Bob wins for λ < 1, and for λ = 1, the game is a draw. But we found that for λ=1, Alice can win by a specific strategy. But we need to prove that for λ < 1, Bazza has a winning strategy. The strategy: at each even turn n, he chooses x_n = sqrt(n - B_{n-1}). This makes B_n = n. We can consider the recurrence for S_{2k} and S_{2k+1}. Let’s define a_k = S_{2k} (after Bazza's move at even 2k), and b_k = S_{2k+1} (after Alice's move at odd 2k+1). Then we have:

a_k = a_{k-1} + x_{2k-1} + sqrt(2 - x_{2k-1}^2), where x_{2k-1} is Alice's move on turn 2k-1, and it must satisfy that b_{k-1}=a_{k-1}+x_{2k-1} ≤ λ(2k-1). Also, we have a_1 = x_1 + sqrt(2 - x_1^2) with x_1 ≤ λ.

Does Bazza have a winning strategy? Let's test numerically with a possible strategy. We need to find a strategy for Bazza that guarantees that Alice eventually cannot move.

We can try to analyze the game using "potential" function. Since the game is symmetric in a sense, we can think of it as a "minimax" problem. Let's try to find a strategy for Bazza when λ<1. Perhaps he can adopt the following: At each even turn, he chooses x_n = 1. Then Alice's linear sum will eventually exceed λ n. But we saw that if λ=0.99, then S after his moves will be x_1 + (k-1). For x_1=0.99, S_{2k}=0.99+(k-1)=k-0.01. Then λ(2k+1)=0.99(2k+1)=1.98k+0.99. For large k, S_{2k} ≈ k, and λ(2k+1) ≈ 1.98k, so S < λ. So she survives. So constant 1 does not kill her.

Maybe Bazza should try to make his moves larger by keeping Q low. But if he makes a larger move, his Q increases, limiting future moves. So he needs to choose a strategy that maximizes the growth of S relative to λ n. This is like a "resource" game where each player has a budget: Alice's budget is λ n, Bazza's budget is n. The moves are alternating. The game can be seen as a "two-player" game where each player's cumulative sum of squares (for Bazza) or linear sum (for Alice) must stay within bounds. This is reminiscent of a "bootstrap" game where the winner is determined by whether λ is less than or greater than 1.

Let's try to think in terms of "critical λ" where the optimal play leads to a draw. Often, for such games, the threshold is where the "optimal" strategy for the player trying to survive is to use a constant strategy. For λ=1, we found that Alice can win by a strategy that forces Bazza's quadratic sum to exceed n. But maybe for λ<1, Bazza can win by forcing Alice's linear sum to exceed λ n. Let's try to find a winning strategy for Bazza for λ<1.

Define the game in terms of the cumulative sums after each turn. Let’s denote the state after an even turn as (A, B) where A = sum x_i, B = sum x_i^2, and the turn number is n. Then Alice's next move at n+1: she chooses x_{n+1} such that A + x_{n+1} ≤ λ (n+1). She can choose any x_{n+1} ≥ 0. Then the state becomes (A + x_{n+1}, B + x_{n+1}^2). Then Bazza's move at n+2: he chooses x_{n+2} such that B + x_{n+2}^2 ≤ n+2. He can choose any x_{n+2} ≥ 0. Then the state becomes (A + x_{n+1} + x_{n+2}, B + x_{n+2}^2). So essentially, the players are adding numbers each turn, with constraints on the cumulative sums.

We can think of the game as: Alice wants to keep A ≤ λ n at the end of her turns; Bazza wants to keep B ≤ n at the end of his turns. They can influence each other's bounds. This is like a "pursuit" game where each player's moves affect the other's bound.

One possible approach: Consider the function F(t) = A_t - λ t, and G(t) = B_t - t. The game ends when either player cannot make a move. For Alice, if A_{t-1} > λ t, then she loses. For Bazza, if B_{t-1} > t, then he loses.

We can try to prove that for λ < 1, Bazza can force that A_t > λ t at some even t (i.e., after his move, A_t is too large for Alice's next turn). But we saw that for λ=0.9, the maximum strategy gave A_{2k} = about 1.414k, and λ(2k+1)=1.8k+0.9, so A_{2k} is less. However, maybe Bazza can adopt a strategy that makes his moves larger than sqrt(2) initially? For λ=0.9, the maximum x_2 is sqrt(2 - x_1^2). If Alice chooses x_1=0.9, then x_2=1.091. That's less than sqrt(2). Then at n=4, he can choose x_4= sqrt(4 - B_3). B_3 depends on x_3. If Alice chooses x_3=0, then x_4= sqrt(2). So his moves are sqrt(2) from then on. So his A_n grows at rate 1.414 per even turn. For λ=0.9, λ n grows as 0.9n, so his A_n is about 1.414n, which is larger than 0.9n, so eventually his A_n will exceed λ n? But we need to compare A_{2k} with λ(2k+1). For large k, A_{2k} ~ 1.414k, λ(2k+1) ~ 1.8k, so A_{2k} < λ(2k+1). So she survives. However, note that the bound for Alice at her turn after Bazza's move is λ n, where n is the turn number. The turn numbers are odd for Alice. So after Bazza's move at n=2k, the next Alice turn is n=2k+1. The condition for Alice to be able to move is A_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1). Since A_{2k} is about 1.414k, and λ(2k+1)=1.8k+0.9, the inequality holds. So she survives.

But maybe we need to consider that Alice might not always choose 0. She might choose positive numbers to reduce the growth of A. For instance, if she chooses a positive x_3, then Bazza's next move x_4 becomes smaller (since sqrt(2-x_3^2) < sqrt(2)), so A_4 is smaller. But that also increases her S_3, which might make her later unable to move. So she might try to balance. This is reminiscent of a "continuous-time" optimal control problem: each player can choose their move to minimize the opponent's future advantage.

We can try to analyze the game using "strategy stealing" or "mirror" ideas. Another approach: Consider the following "potential" function: Define P_n = A_n - λ n. For Alice, she wants to keep P_n ≤ 0 on her turns. For Bazza, he wants to keep Q_n - n ≤ 0 on his turns. If one player can force the other's potential to become positive at some turn, they win.

Maybe we can use a "strategy" where Bazza always chooses x_n = 1 (if possible). Then we can compute the evolution of A_n and Q_n. For λ<1, we need to see if Alice can always survive by choosing appropriate moves. If she can, then Bazza's strategy fails. He can influence A, but also Alice's moves affect Q. He could try to force Alice to choose a positive x on her turn, which would increase her S, but then his next move becomes smaller. However, he might be able to cause her to be forced to exceed λ n at some later turn. This is like a "tug-of-war" where each player can choose their move to minimize the opponent's future advantage.

We need to find a rigorous solution. Let's try to analyze the game using a "potential" function that is a combination of the cumulative sums. Since the constraints involve n, we might consider the "energy" function E_n = A_n - λ n, and also F_n = B_n - n. The game ends when either player cannot move. For Alice, she loses if E_{n-1} > 0? Actually, at her turn with odd n, she needs to choose x_n such that A_n + x_n ≤ λ n, which is equivalent to A_{n-1} + x_n ≤ λ n. If A_{n-1} > λ n, then no x_n can make it ≤ λ n. So she loses if A_{n-1} > λ n. So her condition is A_{n-1} ≤ λ n. For Bazza, he loses if B_{n-1} > n.

Thus, the game is about who can cause the other's cumulative sum to exceed their bound.

We can think of the game as a two-player game where each player can choose a number to add to their own cumulative sum, and the opponent's cumulative sum also changes accordingly. This is reminiscent of a "continuous" game where the optimal strategy is to keep the ratio of the sums constant. Perhaps we can use a "strategy" for Bazza: choose x_n = 1 for all even n, and then show that for λ < 1, Alice will eventually be forced to exceed λ n because her linear sum will increase at a rate of 1 per turn, and her bound is λ n, which grows slower than n if λ<1. But we saw that if she chooses 0, then her linear sum after his moves is x_1 + (k-1). So A_{2k} = x_1 + (k-1). For this to be > λ(2k+1) for some k, we need x_1 + (k-1) > λ(2k+1) => x_1 + k -1 > 2λk + λ => (1-2λ)k > λ - x_1 -1? Actually, rearranging: k -1 - 2λk > λ - x_1 => k(1-2λ) -1 > λ - x_1 => k(1-2λ) > λ - x_1 +1. For λ<1, 1-2λ could be positive if λ<0.5, negative if λ>0.5. For λ=0.9, 1-2λ= -0.8, so the inequality becomes -0.8k > 0.9 - x_1 +1 = 1.9 - x_1. Since left negative, right positive, impossible. So constant 1 doesn't kill her. But if λ<0.5, then 1-2λ>0, and for large k, the left side is positive, so the inequality could hold. For example, λ=0.4, then 1-2λ=0.2, and we need 0.2k > 0.4 - x_1 +1 = 1.4 - x_1. For x_1=0.4, A_{2k}=0.4+(k-1)=k-0.6. λ(2k+1)=0.4(2k+1)=0.8k+0.4. Then inequality k-0.6 > 0.8k+0.4 => -0.6 > 0.8k+0.4? Actually, k-0.6 > 0.8k+0.4 => 0.2k > 1.0 => k>5. So for k=6, A_{12}=5.4? Actually, A_{12}=0.4+5=5.4? Wait, if he chooses 1 each even turn, then after his first move (n=2), A_2= x_1+1 = 1.4; after n=4, A_4=2.4; n=6, 3.4; n=8,4.4; n=10,5.4; n=12,6.4. Wait, 0.4+5=5.4, but we said A_{12}=6.4 earlier? Let's compute properly: x_1=0.4. Then Bazza's first move at n=2: he chooses 1, so A_2=1.4. Then his second move at n=4: A_4=2.4. But earlier we thought that Bazza might have a winning strategy for all λ<1. For λ=0.9, maybe he can use a different constant, say c=1.2? For example, he could try to make his first move t small, so that he can later make a move of 1.4. That gave A_{2k} = x_1 + t + (k-2)*1.4? Let's analyze more generally.

Let’s consider the possibility that Bazza can win for any λ<1. We need to examine the condition for which there exists a strategy for Bazza that ensures that at some even turn, the linear sum A after his move is > λ (next odd turn). This is a kind of "control" problem. Perhaps we can prove that if λ < 1, then Bazza has a winning strategy by using a "strategy of constant" of some value c that is chosen based on λ, but the constant cannot be too large initially, so he might need to "build up" his ability to make larger moves. However, note that his allowed x_n is limited by the budget n - B_{n-1}. If he makes a move of size c at an even turn, then his budget for the quadratic sum after that move is n - (B_{n-1} + c^2) = (n - B_{n-1}) - c^2. Since he used c^2 of his budget, his future allowed moves are smaller. So if he wants to make a large move later, he needs to keep his quadratic sum small. So the optimal strategy for Bazza is to keep his quadratic sum as low as possible, i.e., to always choose the maximum allowed, which makes his quadratic sum exactly n after each even turn. This is the most "aggressive" in terms of growth of A. But as we saw, this may not be sufficient for λ>0.7071. However, maybe Bazza can use a different strategy: he could try to make his moves larger by making Alice's squares larger? But Alice can always choose 0 to keep her squares small. Suppose Bazza wants to maximize the growth of A after his moves, given that Alice always chooses 0. He can choose his moves to increase A, but each move also consumes quadratic budget. The optimal strategy to maximize A after n moves is to use a "continuous" approach: he should make his moves as large as possible, but also he needs to keep his quadratic sum within n. This is like a "resource" problem where he has a budget of n for squares. To maximize the linear sum, he should use the budget efficiently. Given that he wants to maximize the sum of his x_i (which are the even moves) subject to sum x_i^2 ≤ n (the total budget after his turn, but note that n is the turn number, and he has to also consider that the linear sum is not his only output; he also has Alice's moves. Thus, for λ=0.75, Bazza can survive indefinitely. So Alice does not have a winning strategy for λ=0.75. But is it possible that for λ between 0.7071 and 1, Alice could win by a different strategy? Perhaps she could try to make her own linear sum not too tight, but instead use a "constant" strategy to force Bazza's quadratic sum to exceed n. Let's analyze the game in terms of potential functions.

We can consider the following: Define the "energy" E_n = A_n - λ n. For Alice, she wants to keep E_n ≤ 0 on her turns. For Bazza, he wants to keep F_n = B_n - n ≤ 0 on his turns. The game ends when one player cannot move. This is a typical "two-player" game where each player can influence the opponent's potential. There is a known method: consider the function H(t) = A_t - λ t and K(t) = B_t - t. The players alternate moves. The question is: for which λ does the first player (Alice) have a winning strategy? This is similar to the "pursuit" game where the players try to keep their own potential nonpositive. I recall a problem: "Alice and Bob play a game where on each turn, a player chooses a nonnegative number. Alice's constraint: sum ≤ n, Bob's constraint: sum of squares ≤ n. Define g(a) = a^2 + (3λ - a)^2/2 = a^2 + (9λ^2 - 6λ a + a^2)/2 = a^2 + 4.5λ^2 - 3λ a + 0.5a^2 = 1.5a^2 - 3λ a + 4.5λ^2. So g(a) = 1.5a^2 - 3λ a + 4.5λ^2. Now we need to produce a rigorous proof. The solution can be structured as:

1. Show that for λ > (5√2+2√6)/13, Alice can force a win by playing x_1 = 0 (or maybe a specific value). Provide a strategy: She plays x_1 = 0. Then Bazza must choose x_2. She then plays x_3 = 3λ - x_2 (since she will choose the maximum allowed to make Q_3 as large as possible). Actually, she should play x_3 = 3λ - x_2 to make S_3 = 3λ exactly. Then Q_3 = x_2^2 + (3λ - x_2)^2. Then Bazza's next move at n=4: he can choose x_4 up to √(4 - Q_3). Alice will then play x_5 = 5λ - (x_2 + (3λ - x_2) + x_4) = 2λ - x_4. Then Q_5 = Q_3 + x_4^2 + (2λ - x_4)^2. She wants to show that for any x_2, x_4, we have Q_5 > 6. This is the key inequality.
